<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Russian author (1828–1910)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Tolstoy" and "Lev Tolstoy" redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Tolstoy_(disambiguation)" title="Tolstoy (disambiguation)">Tolstoy (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lev_Tolstoy_(disambiguation)" title="Lev Tolstoy (disambiguation)">Lev Tolstoy (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">In this name that follows <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Slavic_naming_customs" title="Eastern Slavic naming customs">Eastern Slavic naming conventions</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a> is <i> Nikolayevich</i> and the <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">family name</a> is <i> Tolstoy</i>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leon_tolstoi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="972" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Leon_tolstoi.jpg/220px-Leon_tolstoi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Leon_tolstoi.jpg/330px-Leon_tolstoi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Leon_tolstoi.jpg/440px-Leon_tolstoi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leon_tolstoi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Leo Tolstoy by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Kramskoi" title="Ivan Kramskoi">Ivan Kramskoi</a>, 1873</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Count" title="Count">Count</a> <b>Lev Nikolayevich Tolstoy</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[note 1]</a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɔɪ/: 'oi' in 'choice'">ɔɪ</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span></span>-/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> Russian: <span lang="ru">Лев Николаевич Толстой</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 2]</a></sup> <small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" lang="ru-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian" title="Help:IPA/Russian">[ˈlʲef nʲɪkɐˈla(j)ɪvʲɪtɕ tɐlˈstoj]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ru-Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9e/Ru-Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy.ogg" title="Ru-Lev Nikolayevich Tolstoy.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; 9 September [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 28 August] 1828 –  20 November [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 7 November] 1910), usually referred to in English as <b>Leo Tolstoy</b>, was a Russian writer who is regarded as one of the greatest authors of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> He received nominations for the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> every year from 1902 to 1906 and for the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> in 1901, 1902, and 1909; the fact that he never won is a major <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_controversies#Literature" title="Nobel Prize controversies">controversy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Born to an aristocratic Russian family in 1828,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> Tolstoy's notable works include the novels <i><a href="/wiki/War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace">War and Peace</a></i> (1869) and <i><a href="/wiki/Anna_Karenina" title="Anna Karenina">Anna Karenina</a></i> (1878),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup> often cited as pinnacles of <a href="/wiki/Literary_realism" title="Literary realism">realist</a> fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> He first achieved literary acclaim in his twenties with his semi-autobiographical trilogy, <i><a href="/wiki/Childhood_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Childhood (Tolstoy novel)">Childhood</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Boyhood_(novel)" title="Boyhood (novel)">Boyhood</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Youth_(Leo_Tolstoy_novel)" title="Youth (Leo Tolstoy novel)">Youth</a></i> (1852–1856), and <i><a href="/wiki/Sevastopol_Sketches" title="Sevastopol Sketches">Sevastopol Sketches</a></i> (1855), based upon his experiences in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>. His fiction includes dozens of short stories and several <a href="/wiki/Novella" title="Novella">novellas</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Ivan_Ilyich" title="The Death of Ivan Ilyich">The Death of Ivan Ilyich</a></i> (1886), <i><a href="/wiki/Family_Happiness" title="Family Happiness">Family Happiness</a></i> (1859), "<a href="/wiki/After_the_Ball_(short_story)" title="After the Ball (short story)">After the Ball</a>" (1911), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadji_Murat_(novel)" title="Hadji Murat (novel)">Hadji Murad</a></i> (1912). He also wrote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Play_(theater)" title="Play (theater)">plays</a> and numerous philosophical essays.
</p><p>In the 1870s, Tolstoy experienced a profound moral crisis, followed by what he regarded as an equally profound spiritual awakening, as outlined in his non-fiction work <i><a href="/wiki/A_Confession" title="A Confession">A Confession</a></i> (1882). His literal interpretation of the ethical teachings of Jesus, centering on the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, caused him to become a fervent <a href="/wiki/Christian_anarchism" title="Christian anarchism">Christian anarchist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-pacifist" title="Anarcho-pacifist">pacifist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> His ideas on <a href="/wiki/Nonviolent_resistance" title="Nonviolent resistance">nonviolent resistance</a>, expressed in such works as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kingdom_of_God_Is_Within_You" title="The Kingdom of God Is Within You">The Kingdom of God Is Within You</a></i> (1894), had a profound impact on such pivotal 20th-century figures as <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ResistNotEvil_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ResistNotEvil-11">[9]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> He also became a dedicated advocate of <a href="/wiki/Georgism" title="Georgism">Georgism</a>, the economic philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>, which he incorporated into his writing, particularly <i><a href="/wiki/Resurrection_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Resurrection (Tolstoy novel)">Resurrection</a></i> (1899).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tolstoy_family" title="Tolstoy family">Tolstoy family</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tolstoy_family" title="Tolstoy family">Tolstoys</a> were a well-known family of old <a href="/wiki/Russian_nobility" title="Russian nobility">Russian nobility</a> who traced their ancestry to a mythical nobleman named Indris described by <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Andreyevich_Tolstoy" title="Pyotr Andreyevich Tolstoy">Pyotr Tolstoy</a> as arriving "from Nemec, from the lands of <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Caesar</a>" to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernigov" title="Chernigov">Chernigov</a> in 1353 along with his two sons Litvinos (or Litvonis) and Zimonten (or Zigmont) and a <a href="/wiki/Druzhina" title="Druzhina">druzhina</a> of 3000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rummel_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-rummel-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bunin_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-bunin-14">[12]</a></sup> While the word "Nemec" has been long used to describe Germans only, at that time it was applied to any foreigner who didn't speak Russian (from the word <i>nemoy</i> meaning <i>mute</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> Indris was then converted to <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>, under the name of Leonty, and his sons as Konstantin and Feodor. Konstantin's grandson Andrei Kharitonovich was nicknamed Tolstiy (translated as <i>fat</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Vasily_II_of_Moscow" title="Vasily II of Moscow">Vasily II of Moscow</a> after he moved from Chernigov to Moscow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rummel_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-rummel-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bunin_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-bunin-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the pagan names and the fact that Chernigov at the time was ruled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_I_Starshy" title="Demetrius I Starshy">Demetrius I Starshy</a>, some researchers concluded that they were <a href="/wiki/Lithuanians" title="Lithuanians">Lithuanians</a> who arrived from the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rummel_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-rummel-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> At the same time, no mention of Indris was ever found in the 14th-to-16th-century documents, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Russian_Chronicles" title="Old Russian Chronicles">Chernigov Chronicles</a> used by Pyotr Tolstoy as a reference were lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rummel_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-rummel-13">[11]</a></sup> The first documented members of the Tolstoy family also lived during the 17th century, thus Pyotr Tolstoy himself is generally considered the founder of the noble house, being granted the title of <a href="/wiki/Count" title="Count">count</a> by <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="923" data-file-width="679" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg/220px-Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg/330px-Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg/440px-Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lev_Nikolayevich_Tolstoy_1848.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Leo Tolstoy at age 20, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1848</div></div></div>
<p>Tolstoy was born at <a href="/wiki/Yasnaya_Polyana" title="Yasnaya Polyana">Yasnaya Polyana</a>, a family estate 12 kilometres (7.5 mi) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Tula,_Russia" title="Tula, Russia">Tula</a>, and 200 kilometres (120 mi) south of Moscow. He was the fourth of five children of <a href="/wiki/Count" title="Count">Count</a> Nikolai Ilyich Tolstoy (1794–1837), a veteran of the <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">Patriotic War of 1812</a>, and Princess Mariya Tolstaya (née <a href="/wiki/Volkonsky" title="Volkonsky">Volkonskaya</a>; 1790–1830). His mother died when he was two and his father when he was nine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthorDataSheet_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AuthorDataSheet-20">[18]</a></sup> Tolstoy and his siblings were brought up by relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> In 1844, he began studying law and oriental languages at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kazan_University" title="Kazan University">Kazan University</a>, where teachers described him as "both unable and unwilling to learn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthorDataSheet_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-AuthorDataSheet-20">[18]</a></sup> Tolstoy left the university in the middle of his studies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthorDataSheet_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-AuthorDataSheet-20">[18]</a></sup> returned to Yasnaya Polyana and then spent much time in Moscow, Tula and Saint Petersburg, leading a lax and leisurely lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> He began writing during this period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthorDataSheet_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-AuthorDataSheet-20">[18]</a></sup> including his first novel <i><a href="/wiki/Childhood_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Childhood (Tolstoy novel)">Childhood</a></i>, a fictitious account of his own youth, which was published in 1852.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> In 1851, after running up heavy gambling debts, he went with his older brother to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasus_(geographic_region)" title="Caucasus (geographic region)">Caucasus</a> and joined the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Army" title="Imperial Russian Army">army</a>. Tolstoy served as a young artillery officer during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> and was in Sevastopol during the 11-month-long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sevastopol_(1854%E2%80%9355)" title="Siege of Sevastopol (1854–55)">siege of Sevastopol</a> in 1854–55,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCTen_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCTen-21">[19]</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Chernaya" title="Battle of the Chernaya">Battle of the Chernaya</a>. During the war he was recognised for his courage and promoted to lieutenant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCTen_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCTen-21">[19]</a></sup> He was appalled by the number of deaths involved in warfare,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthorDataSheet_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-AuthorDataSheet-20">[18]</a></sup> and left the army after the end of the Crimean War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>His experience in the army, and two trips around Europe in 1857 and 1860–61 converted Tolstoy from a dissolute and privileged society author to a non-violent and spiritual <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchist</a>. Others who followed the same path were <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Herzen" title="Alexander Herzen">Alexander Herzen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a>. During his 1857 visit, Tolstoy witnessed a public execution in Paris, a traumatic experience that marked the rest of his life. In a letter to his friend <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Botkin" title="Vasily Botkin">Vasily Botkin</a>, Tolstoy wrote: "The truth is that the State is a conspiracy designed not only to exploit, but above all to corrupt its citizens ... Henceforth, I shall never serve any government anywhere."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> Tolstoy's concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-violence" title="Non-violence">non-violence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa" title="Ahimsa">ahimsa</a> was bolstered when he read a <a href="/wiki/Tirukkural_translations_into_German" title="Tirukkural translations into German">German version</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tirukkural" title="Tirukkural">Tirukkural</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PearlsOfInsp_Rajaram_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-PearlsOfInsp_Rajaram-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsh2018_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walsh2018-24">[22]</a></sup> He later instilled the concept in <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> through his <a href="/wiki/A_Letter_to_a_Hindu" title="A Letter to a Hindu">A Letter to a Hindu</a> when young Gandhi corresponded with him seeking his advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsh2018_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walsh2018-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gandhi_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-gandhi-26">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>His European trip in 1860–61 shaped both his political and literary development when he met <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>. Tolstoy read Hugo's newly finished <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Mis%C3%A9rables" title="Les Misérables">Les Misérables</a></i>. The similar evocation of battle scenes in Hugo's novel and Tolstoy's <i><a href="/wiki/War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace">War and Peace</a></i> indicates this influence. Tolstoy's political philosophy was also influenced by a March 1861 visit to French anarchist <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a>, then living in exile under an assumed name in Brussels. Tolstoy reviewed Proudhon's forthcoming publication, <i>La Guerre et la Paix</i> ("<i>War and Peace</i>" in French), and later used the title for his masterpiece. The two men also discussed education, as Tolstoy wrote in his educational notebooks: "If I recount this conversation with Proudhon, it is to show that, in my personal experience, he was the only man who understood the significance of education and of the printing press in our time."
</p><p>Fired by enthusiasm, Tolstoy returned to Yasnaya Polyana and founded 13 schools for the children of Russia's peasants, who had just been <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_reform_of_1861" title="Emancipation reform of 1861">emancipated from serfdom in 1861</a>. Tolstoy described the schools' principles in his 1862 essay "The School at Yasnaya Polyana".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> His educational experiments were short-lived, partly due to harassment by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Tsarist</a> secret police. However, as a direct forerunner to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.S._Neill" title="A.S. Neill">A.S. Neill</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Summerhill_School" title="Summerhill School">Summerhill School</a>, the school at Yasnaya Polyana<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> can justifiably be claimed the first example of a coherent theory of <a href="/wiki/Democratic_education" title="Democratic education">democratic education</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<p>The death of his brother Nikolay in 1860 had an impact on Tolstoy, and led him to a desire to marry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthorDataSheet_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-AuthorDataSheet-20">[18]</a></sup> On 23 September 1862, Tolstoy married <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Tolstaya" title="Sophia Tolstaya">Sophia Andreevna Behrs</a>, who was sixteen years his junior and the daughter of a court physician. She was called Sonya, the Russian diminutive of Sofia, by her family and friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-29">[27]</a></sup> They had 13 children, eight of whom survived childhood:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="884" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="374" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg/220px-Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg/330px-Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg/440px-Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ge_Sophia_Tolstaya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tolstoy's wife <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Tolstaya" title="Sophia Tolstaya">Sophia</a> and their daughter <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Tolstaya" title="Alexandra Tolstaya">Alexandra</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sergei_Tolstoy" title="Sergei Tolstoy">Count Sergei Lvovich Tolstoy</a> (1863–1947), composer and ethnomusicologist</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tatyana_Sukhotina-Tolstaya" title="Tatyana Sukhotina-Tolstaya">Countess Tatyana Lvovna Tolstaya</a> (1864–1950), wife of Mikhail Sergeevich Sukhotin</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ilya_Tolstoy" title="Ilya Tolstoy">Count Ilya Lvovich Tolstoy</a> (1866–1933), writer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lev_Lvovich_Tolstoy" title="Lev Lvovich Tolstoy">Count Lev Lvovich Tolstoy</a> (1869–1945), writer and sculptor</li>
<li>Countess Maria Lvovna Tolstaya (1871–1906), wife of <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Leonidovich_Obolensky" title="Nikolai Leonidovich Obolensky">Nikolai Leonidovich Obolensky</a></li>
<li>Count Peter Lvovich Tolstoy (1872–1873), died in infancy</li>
<li>Count Nikolai Lvovich Tolstoy (1874–1875), died in infancy</li>
<li>Countess Varvara Lvovna Tolstaya (1875–1875), died in infancy</li>
<li>Count Andrei Lvovich Tolstoy (1877–1916), served in the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a></li>
<li>Count Michael Lvovich Tolstoy (1879–1944)</li>
<li>Count Alexei Lvovich Tolstoy (1881–1886)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Tolstaya" title="Alexandra Tolstaya">Countess Alexandra Lvovna Tolstaya</a> (1884–1979)</li>
<li>Count Ivan Lvovich Tolstoy (1888–1895)</li></ul>
<p>The marriage was marked from the outset by sexual passion and emotional insensitivity when Tolstoy, on the eve of their marriage, gave her his diaries detailing his extensive sexual past and the fact that one of the serfs on his estate had borne him a son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-29">[27]</a></sup> Even so, their early married life was happy and allowed Tolstoy much freedom and the support system to compose <i>War and Peace</i> and <i>Anna Karenina</i> with Sonya acting as his secretary, editor, and financial manager. Sonya was copying and hand-writing his epic works time after time. Tolstoy would continue editing <i>War and Peace</i> and had to have clean final drafts to be delivered to the publisher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, their later life together has been described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.N._Wilson" title="A.N. Wilson">A.N. Wilson</a> as one of the unhappiest in literary history. Tolstoy's relationship with his wife deteriorated as his beliefs became increasingly radical. This saw him seeking to reject his inherited and earned wealth, including the renunciation of the copyrights on his earlier works.
</p><p>Some of the members of the Tolstoy family left Russia in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/1905_Russian_Revolution" title="1905 Russian Revolution">1905 Russian Revolution</a> and the subsequent establishment of the Soviet Union, and many of Leo Tolstoy's relatives and descendants today live in Sweden, Germany, the United Kingdom, France and the United States. Tolstoy's son, Count <a href="/wiki/Lev_Lvovich_Tolstoy" title="Lev Lvovich Tolstoy">Lev Lvovich Tolstoy</a>, settled in Sweden and married a Swedish woman. Leo Tolstoy's last surviving grandchild, Countess <a href="/wiki/Tatiana_Tolstoy-Paus" title="Tatiana Tolstoy-Paus">Tatiana Tolstoy-Paus</a>, died in 2007 at <a href="/wiki/Herresta" title="Herresta">Herresta</a> manor in Sweden, which is owned by Tolstoy's descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> Swedish jazz singer <a href="/wiki/Viktoria_Tolstoy" title="Viktoria Tolstoy">Viktoria Tolstoy</a> is also descended from Leo Tolstoy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of his great-great-grandsons, Vladimir Tolstoy (born 1962), is a director of the <a href="/wiki/Yasnaya_Polyana" title="Yasnaya Polyana">Yasnaya Polyana</a> museum since 1994 and an adviser to the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Russia" title="President of Russia">President of Russia</a> on cultural affairs since 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Tolstoy" title="Ilya Tolstoy">Ilya Tolstoy</a>'s great-grandson, <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Olegovich_Tolstoy" title="Pyotr Olegovich Tolstoy">Pyotr Tolstoy</a>, is a well-known Russian journalist and TV presenter as well as a <a href="/wiki/7th_State_Duma" title="7th State Duma">State Duma</a> deputy since 2016. His cousin Fyokla Tolstaya (born Anna Tolstaya in 1971), daughter of the acclaimed Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavist" title="Slavist">Slavist</a> Nikita Tolstoy (<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%81%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%B9,_%D0%9D%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B0_%D0%98%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B8%D1%87" title="ru:Толстой, Никита Ильич">ru</a>) (1923–1996), is also a Russian journalist, TV and radio host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Novels_and_fictional_works">Novels and fictional works</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="1046" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg/220px-Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg/330px-Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg/440px-Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tolstoy_Leo_port.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tolstoy in 1906</div></div></div>
<p>Tolstoy is considered one of the giants of Russian literature; his works include the novels <i><a href="/wiki/War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace">War and Peace</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Anna_Karenina" title="Anna Karenina">Anna Karenina</a></i> and novellas such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadji_Murat_(novel)" title="Hadji Murat (novel)">Hadji Murad</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Ivan_Ilyich" title="The Death of Ivan Ilyich">The Death of Ivan Ilyich</a></i>.
</p><p>Tolstoy's earliest works, the autobiographical novels <a href="/wiki/Childhood_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Childhood (Tolstoy novel)"><i>Childhood</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Boyhood_(novel)" title="Boyhood (novel)"><i>Boyhood</i></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Youth_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Youth (Tolstoy novel)"><i>Youth</i></a> (1852–1856), tell of a rich landowner's son and his slow realization of the chasm between himself and his peasants. Though he later rejected them as sentimental, a great deal of Tolstoy's own life is revealed. They retain their relevance as accounts of the universal story of growing up.
</p><p>Tolstoy served as a <a href="/wiki/Second_lieutenant" title="Second lieutenant">second lieutenant</a> in an artillery regiment during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, recounted in his <i><a href="/wiki/Sevastopol_Sketches" title="Sevastopol Sketches">Sevastopol Sketches</a></i>. His experiences in battle helped stir his subsequent <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> and gave him material for realistic depiction of the horrors of war in his later work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>His fiction consistently attempts to convey realistically the Russian society in which he lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cossacks_(novel)" title="The Cossacks (novel)">The Cossacks</a></i> (1863) describes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cossack" title="Cossack">Cossack</a> life and people through a story of a Russian aristocrat in love with a Cossack girl. <i>Anna Karenina</i> (1877) tells parallel stories of an adulterous woman trapped by the conventions and falsities of society and of a philosophical landowner (much like Tolstoy), who works alongside the peasants in the fields and seeks to reform their lives. Tolstoy not only drew from his own life experiences but also created characters in his own image, such as Pierre Bezukhov and Prince Andrei in <i>War and Peace</i>, Levin in <i>Anna Karenina</i> and to some extent, Prince Nekhlyudov in <i>Resurrection</i>. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Pevear_and_Larissa_Volokhonsky" title="Richard Pevear and Larissa Volokhonsky">Richard Pevear</a>, who translated many of Tolstoy's works, said of Tolstoy’s signature style, "His works are full of provocation and irony, and written with broad and elaborately developed rhetorical devices."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2417-Peralta.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="4349" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2417-Peralta.jpg/200px-Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2417-Peralta.jpg" class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2465-Peralta.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="4336" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2465-Peralta.jpg/200px-Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2465-Peralta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2465-Peralta.jpg/300px-Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2465-Peralta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2465-Peralta.jpg/400px-Die_Macht_der_Finsternis_2465-Peralta.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">2015 at Vienna's <a href="/wiki/Akademietheater" title="Akademietheater">Akademietheater</a></div></div></div></div>
<p><i>War and Peace</i> is generally thought to be one of the greatest novels ever written, remarkable for its dramatic breadth and unity. Its vast canvas includes 580 characters, many historical with others fictional. The story moves from family life to the headquarters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a>, from the court of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Alexander I of Russia</a> to the battlefields of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">Austerlitz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Borodino" title="Battle of Borodino">Borodino</a>. Tolstoy's original idea for the novel was to investigate the causes of the <a href="/wiki/Decembrist_revolt" title="Decembrist revolt">Decembrist revolt</a>, to which it refers only in the last chapters, from which can be deduced that <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Nikolayevich_Bolkonsky" title="Andrei Nikolayevich Bolkonsky">Andrei Bolkonsky</a>'s son will become one of the Decembrists. The novel explores Tolstoy's theory of history, and in particular the insignificance of individuals such as Napoleon and Alexander. Somewhat surprisingly, Tolstoy did not consider <i>War and Peace</i> to be a novel (nor did he consider many of the great Russian fictions written at that time to be novels). This view becomes less surprising if one considers that Tolstoy was a novelist of the <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realist</a> school who considered the novel to be a framework for the examination of social and political issues in nineteenth-century life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> <i>War and Peace</i> (which is to Tolstoy really an <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic</a> in prose) therefore did not qualify. Tolstoy thought that <i>Anna Karenina</i> was his first true novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>After <i>Anna Karenina</i>, Tolstoy concentrated on Christian themes, and his later novels such as <i>The Death of Ivan Ilyich</i> (1886) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/What_Is_to_Be_Done%3F_(Tolstoy)" title="What Is to Be Done? (Tolstoy)">What Is to Be Done?</a></i> develop a radical <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-pacifism" title="Anarcho-pacifism">anarcho-pacifist</a> Christian philosophy which led to his <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> in 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> For all the praise showered on <i>Anna Karenina</i> and <i>War and Peace</i>, Tolstoy rejected the two works later in his life as something not as true of reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his novel <i><a href="/wiki/Resurrection_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Resurrection (Tolstoy novel)">Resurrection</a></i>, Tolstoy attempts to expose the injustice of man-made laws and the hypocrisy of an institutionalized church. Tolstoy also explores and explains the economic philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Georgism" title="Georgism">Georgism</a>, of which he had become a very strong advocate towards the end of his life.
</p><p>Tolstoy also tried himself in poetry, with several soldier songs written during his military service, and fairy tales in verse such as <i>Volga-bogatyr</i> and <i>Oaf</i> stylized as national folk songs. They were written between 1871 and 1874 for his <i>Russian Book for Reading</i>, a collection of short stories in four volumes (total of 629 stories in various genres) published along with the <i>New Azbuka</i> textbook and addressed to schoolchildren. Nevertheless, he was skeptical about poetry as a genre. As he famously said, "Writing poetry is like ploughing and dancing at the same time." According to <a href="/wiki/Valentin_Bulgakov" title="Valentin Bulgakov">Valentin Bulgakov</a>, he criticised poets, including <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pushkin" title="Alexander Pushkin">Alexander Pushkin</a>, for their "false" epithets used "simply to make it rhyme."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_appraisal_by_other_authors">Critical appraisal by other authors</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tolstoy_1899.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="328" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tolstoy_1899.jpg/180px-Tolstoy_1899.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tolstoy_1899.jpg/270px-Tolstoy_1899.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Tolstoy_1899.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tolstoy_1899.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Captioned "War and Peace", caricature of Tolstoy in the London magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(British_magazine)" title="Vanity Fair (British magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i>, February 1901</div></div></div>
<p>Tolstoy's contemporaries paid him lofty tributes. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>, who died thirty years before Tolstoy, admired and was delighted by Tolstoy's novels (and, conversely, Tolstoy also admired Dostoyevsky's work).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dosteoevsky_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dosteoevsky-46">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">Gustave Flaubert</a>, on reading a translation of <i>War and Peace</i>, exclaimed, "What an artist and what a psychologist!" <a href="/wiki/Anton_Chekhov" title="Anton Chekhov">Anton Chekhov</a>, who often visited Tolstoy at his country estate, wrote, "When literature possesses a Tolstoy, it is easy and pleasant to be a writer; even when you know you have achieved nothing yourself and are still achieving nothing, this is not as terrible as it might otherwise be, because Tolstoy achieves for everyone. What he does serves to justify all the hopes and aspirations invested in literature." The 19th-century British poet and critic <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a> opined that "A novel by Tolstoy is not a work of art but a piece of life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Babel" title="Isaac Babel">Isaac Babel</a> said that "if the world could write by itself, it would write like Tolstoy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later novelists continued to appreciate Tolstoy's art, but sometimes also expressed criticism. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle</a> wrote, "I am attracted by his earnestness and by his power of detail, but I am repelled by his looseness of construction and by his unreasonable and impracticable mysticism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACD_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACD-47">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> declared him "the greatest of all novelists."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a> noted that, "He is never dull, never stupid, never tired, never pedantic, never theatrical!" <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a> wrote of Tolstoy's seemingly guileless artistry: "Seldom did art work so much like nature." <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a> heaped superlatives upon <i><a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Ivan_Ilyich" title="The Death of Ivan Ilyich">The Death of Ivan Ilyich</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Anna_Karenina" title="Anna Karenina">Anna Karenina</a></i>; he questioned, however, the reputation of <i><a href="/wiki/War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace">War and Peace</a></i>, and sharply criticized <i><a href="/wiki/Resurrection_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Resurrection (Tolstoy novel)">Resurrection</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kreutzer_Sonata" title="The Kreutzer Sonata">The Kreutzer Sonata</a></i>. Critic <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bloom" title="Harold Bloom">Harold Bloom</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadji_Murat_(novella)" title="Hadji Murat (novella)">Hadji Murat</a></i> "my personal touchstone for the sublime in prose fiction, to me the best story in the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p>

portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Anarchism_sidebar" title="Template:Anarchism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Anarchism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Anarchism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anarchism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6490" data-file-width="2281" decoding="async" height="484" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tolstoy dressed in peasant clothing, by <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Repin" title="Ilya Repin">Ilya Repin</a> (1901)</div></div></div>
<p>After reading <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_as_Will_and_Representation" title="The World as Will and Representation">The World as Will and Representation</a></i>, Tolstoy gradually became converted to the ascetic morality upheld in that work as the proper spiritual path for the upper classes. In 1869 he writes: "Do you know what this summer has meant for me? Constant raptures over Schopenhauer and a whole series of spiritual delights which I've never experienced before....no student has ever studied so much on his course, and learned so much, as I have this summer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Tolstoy_in_the_hell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="463" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Leo_Tolstoy_in_the_hell.jpg/250px-Leo_Tolstoy_in_the_hell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Leo_Tolstoy_in_the_hell.jpg/375px-Leo_Tolstoy_in_the_hell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/81/Leo_Tolstoy_in_the_hell.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Tolstoy_in_the_hell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Leo Tolstoy in Hell. Collection of the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_History_of_Religion" title="Museum of the History of Religion">Museum of the History of Religion</a> and Atheism. 1883 On a fragment of a wall painting from the church of St. Tazov <a href="/wiki/Kursk_Governorate" title="Kursk Governorate">Kursk Governorate</a> Tolstoy is depicted in the arms of Satan</div></div></div><p>
In Chapter VI of <i><a href="/wiki/A_Confession" title="A Confession">A Confession</a></i>, Tolstoy quoted the final paragraph of Schopenhauer's work. It explains how a complete <a href="/wiki/Self-denial" title="Self-denial">denial of self</a> causes only a relative nothingness which is not to be feared. Tolstoy was struck by the description of Christian, Buddhist, and Hindu ascetic renunciation as being the path to holiness. After reading passages such as the following, which abound in Schopenhauer's ethical chapters, the Russian nobleman chose poverty and formal denial of the will: </p><blockquote><p> But this very necessity of involuntary suffering (by poor people) for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eternal_salvation" title="Eternal salvation">eternal salvation</a> is also expressed by that utterance of the Savior (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matthew_19:24" title="Matthew 19:24">Matthew 19:24</a>): "It is easier for a camel to go through the eye of a needle, than for a rich man to enter into the kingdom of God." Therefore, those who were greatly in earnest about their eternal salvation, chose <a href="/wiki/Simple_living" title="Simple living">voluntary poverty</a> when fate had denied this to them and they had been born in wealth. Thus Buddha <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakyamuni" title="Sakyamuni">Sakyamuni</a> was born a prince, but voluntarily took to the mendicant's staff; and <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">Francis of Assisi</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Mendicant_orders" title="Mendicant orders">mendicant orders</a> who, as a youngster at a ball, where the daughters of all the notabilities were sitting together, was asked: "Now Francis, will you not soon make your choice from these beauties?" and who replied: "I have made a far more beautiful choice!" "Whom?" "<i>La povertà</i> (poverty)": whereupon he abandoned every thing shortly afterwards and wandered through the land as a mendicant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In 1884, Tolstoy wrote a book called <i>What I Believe</i>, in which he openly confessed his Christian beliefs. He affirmed his belief in Jesus Christ's teachings and was particularly influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, and the injunction to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turn_the_other_cheek" title="Turn the other cheek">turn the other cheek</a>, which he understood as a "commandment of non-resistance to evil by force" and a doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">nonviolence</a>. In his work <i>The Kingdom of God Is Within You</i>, he explains that he considered mistaken the Church's doctrine because they had made a "perversion" of Christ's teachings. Tolstoy also received letters from American <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a> who introduced him to the non-violence writings of Quaker Christians such as <a href="/wiki/George_Fox" title="George Fox">George Fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Dymond" title="Jonathan Dymond">Jonathan Dymond</a>. Tolstoy believed being a Christian required him to be a pacifist; the apparently inevitable waging of war by governments is why he is considered a philosophical anarchist.
</p><p>Later, various versions of "Tolstoy's Bible" were published, indicating the passages Tolstoy most relied on, specifically, the reported words of Jesus himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="479" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg/220px-Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg/330px-Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg/440px-Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Tolstoy_Farm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi" title="Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi">Mohandas K. Gandhi</a> and other residents of <a href="/wiki/Tolstoy_Farm" title="Tolstoy Farm">Tolstoy Farm</a>, South Africa, 1910</div></div></div>
<p>Tolstoy believed that a true Christian could find lasting happiness by striving for inner perfection through following the <a href="/wiki/Great_Commandment" title="Great Commandment">Great Commandment</a> of loving one's neighbor and God, rather than guidance from the Church or state. Another distinct attribute of his philosophy based on Christ's teachings is <a href="/wiki/Nonresistance" title="Nonresistance">nonresistance</a> during conflict. This idea in Tolstoy's book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kingdom_of_God_Is_Within_You" title="The Kingdom of God Is Within You">The Kingdom of God Is Within You</a></i> directly influenced <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> and therefore also nonviolent resistance movements to this day.
</p><p>Tolstoy believed that the aristocracy was a burden on the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> He opposed <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private</a> land ownership and the institution of marriage, and valued chastity and sexual abstinence (discussed in <i><a href="/wiki/Father_Sergius" title="Father Sergius">Father Sergius</a></i> and his preface to <i>The Kreutzer Sonata</i>), ideals also held by the young Gandhi. Tolstoy's passion from the depth of his austere moral views is reflected in his later work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> One example is the sequence of the temptation of Sergius in <i>Father Sergius</i>. <a href="/wiki/Maxim_Gorky" title="Maxim Gorky">Maxim Gorky</a> relates how Tolstoy once read this passage before him and Chekhov, and Tolstoy was moved to tears by the end of the reading. Later passages of rare power include the personal crises faced by the protagonists of <i>The Death of Ivan Ilyich</i>, and of <i><a href="/wiki/Master_and_Man_(short_story)" title="Master and Man (short story)">Master and Man</a></i>, where the main character in the former and the reader in the latter are made aware of the foolishness of the protagonists' lives.
</p><p>In 1886, Tolstoy wrote to the Russian <a href="/wiki/List_of_Russian_explorers" title="List of Russian explorers">explorer</a> and anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Miklouho-Maclay" title="Nicholas Miklouho-Maclay">Nicholas Miklouho-Maclay</a>, who was one of the first anthropologists to refute <a href="/wiki/Polygenism" title="Polygenism">polygenism</a>, the view that the different <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)" title="Race (human categorization)">races of mankind</a> belonged to different species: "You were the first to demonstrate beyond question by your experience that man is man everywhere, that is, a kind, sociable being with whom communication can and should be established through kindness and truth, not guns and spirits."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Tolstoy had a profound influence on the development of <a href="/wiki/Christian_anarchism" title="Christian anarchism">Christian anarchist</a> thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolstoyan" title="Tolstoyan">Tolstoyans</a> were a small Christian anarchist group formed by Tolstoy's companion, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Chertkov" title="Vladimir Chertkov">Vladimir Chertkov</a> (1854–1936), to spread Tolstoy's religious teachings. From 1892 he regularly met with the student-activist <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Maklakov" title="Vasily Maklakov">Vasily Maklakov</a> who would defend several Tolstoyans; they discussed the fate of the <a href="/wiki/Doukhobors" title="Doukhobors">Doukhobors</a>. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a> wrote of Tolstoy in the article on anarchism in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1911_Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="1911 Encyclopædia Britannica">1911 Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i>:</p><blockquote><p>Without naming himself an anarchist, Leo Tolstoy, like his predecessors in the popular religious movements of the 15th and 16th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Petr_Chel%C4%8Dick%C3%BD" title="Petr Chelčický">Chojecki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Denck" title="Hans Denck">Denk</a> and many others, took the anarchist position as regards the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Property_right" title="Property right">property rights</a>, deducing his conclusions from the general spirit of the teachings of Jesus and from the necessary dictates of reason. With all the might of his talent, Tolstoy made (especially in <i>The Kingdom of God Is Within You</i>) a powerful criticism of the church, the state and law altogether, and especially of the present <a href="/wiki/Property_law" title="Property law">property laws</a>. He describes the state as the domination of the wicked ones, supported by brutal force. Robbers, he says, are far less dangerous than a well-organized government. He makes a searching criticism of the prejudices which are current now concerning the benefits conferred upon men by the church, the state, and the existing distribution of property, and from the teachings of Jesus he deduces the rule of non-resistance and the absolute condemnation of all wars. His religious arguments are, however, so well combined with arguments borrowed from a dispassionate observation of the present evils, that the anarchist portions of his works appeal to the religious and the non-religious reader alike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara,_1891.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="316" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara%2C_1891.jpg/220px-Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara%2C_1891.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara%2C_1891.jpg/330px-Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara%2C_1891.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara%2C_1891.jpg/440px-Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara%2C_1891.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tolstoy_organising_famine_relief_in_Samara,_1891.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tolstoy organising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_famine_of_1891%E2%80%9392" title="Russian famine of 1891–92">famine relief</a> in Samara, 1891</div></div></div>
<p>Tolstoy denounced the intervention by the <a href="/wiki/Eight-Nation_Alliance" title="Eight-Nation Alliance">Eight-Nation Alliance</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orwin2002_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orwin2002-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolstoy(graf)1978_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tolstoy(graf)1978-58">[56]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">Filipino-American War</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tolstoy praised the Boxer Rebellion and harshly criticized the atrocities of the Russian, German, American, Japanese, and other troops of the Eight-Nation alliance. He heard about the looting, rapes, and murders, and accused the troops of slaughter and "Christian brutality." He named the monarchs most responsible for the atrocities as <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Tsar Nicholas II</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_Germany" title="Wilhelm II of Germany">Kaiser Wilhelm II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> He described the intervention as "terrible for its injustice and cruelty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenStachel1974_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-CohenStachel1974-62">[60]</a></sup> The war was also criticized by other intellectuals such as <a href="/wiki/Leonid_Andreyev" title="Leonid Andreyev">Leonid Andreyev</a> and Gorky. As part of the criticism, Tolstoy wrote an epistle called <i>To the Chinese people</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gamsa2008_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gamsa2008-63">[61]</a></sup> In 1902, he wrote an open letter describing and denouncing Nicholas II's activities in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FlathSmith2011_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FlathSmith2011-64">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Boxer Rebellion stirred Tolstoy's interest in Chinese philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodde1967_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodde1967-65">[63]</a></sup> He was a famous <a href="/wiki/Sinophile" title="Sinophile">sinophile</a>, and read the works of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> and Lao Zi. Tolstoy wrote <i>Chinese Wisdom</i> and other texts about China. Tolstoy corresponded with the Chinese intellectual <a href="/wiki/Gu_Hongming" title="Gu Hongming">Gu Hongming</a> and recommended that China remain an agrarian nation, and not reform like Japan. Tolstoy and Gu opposed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hundred_Day%27s_Reform" title="Hundred Day's Reform">Hundred Day's Reform</a> by <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a> and believed that the reform movement was perilous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee2005_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lee2005-69">[67]</a></sup> Tolstoy's ideology of non-violence shaped the thought of the Chinese anarchist group Society for the Study of Socialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2009_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2009-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="265" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Leo_Tolstoy-1908.webm" height="165" id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Leo_Tolstoy-1908.webm/220px--Leo_Tolstoy-1908.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="563216" data-framerate="1000" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="480" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/16/Leo_Tolstoy-1908.webm/Leo_Tolstoy-1908.webm.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Tolstoy-1908.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Film by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Drankov" title="Alexander Drankov">Aleksandr Osipovich Drankov</a> of Tolstoy's 80th birthday (1908) at Yasnaya Polyana, showing his wife Sofya (picking flowers in the garden) daughter Aleksandra (sitting in the carriage in the white blouse); his aide and confidante V. Chertkov (bald man with the beard and mustache); and students.</div></div></div>
<p>In hundreds of essays over the last 20 years of his life, Tolstoy reiterated the anarchist critique of the state and recommended books by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kropotkin" title="Kropotkin">Kropotkin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proudhon" title="Proudhon">Proudhon</a> to his readers, while rejecting anarchism's espousal of <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_of_the_deed" title="Propaganda of the deed">violent revolutionary means</a>. In the 1900 essay, "On Anarchy,” he wrote: "The Anarchists are right in everything; in the negation of the existing order, and in the assertion that, without Authority, there could not be worse violence than that of Authority under existing conditions. They are mistaken only in thinking that Anarchy can be instituted by a revolution. But it will be instituted only by there being more and more people who do not require the protection of governmental power ... There can be only one permanent revolution – a moral one: the regeneration of the inner man." Despite his misgivings about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist_violence" title="Anarchist violence">anarchist violence</a>, Tolstoy took risks to circulate the prohibited publications of <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_Russia" title="Anarchism in Russia">anarchist thinkers in Russia</a>, and corrected the proofs of Kropotkin's "Words of a Rebel", illegally published in St Petersburg in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2709" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg/220px-Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg/330px-Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg/440px-Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Tolstoi_v_kabinetie.05.1908.ws.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tolstoy in his study in 1908 (age 80)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1908, Tolstoy wrote <i><a href="/wiki/A_Letter_to_a_Hindu" title="A Letter to a Hindu">A Letter to a Hindu</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> outlining his belief in non-violence as a means for India to gain <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">independence</a> from <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">colonial rule</a>. In 1909, Gandhi read a copy of the letter when he was becoming an activist in South Africa. He wrote to Tolstoy seeking proof that he was the author, which led to further correspondence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PearlsOfInsp_Rajaram_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-PearlsOfInsp_Rajaram-23">[21]</a></sup> Tolstoy's <i>The Kingdom of God Is Within You</i> also helped to convince Gandhi of <a href="/wiki/Nonviolent_resistance" title="Nonviolent resistance">nonviolent resistance</a>, a debt Gandhi acknowledged in his autobiography, calling Tolstoy "the greatest apostle of non-violence that the present age has produced". Their correspondence lasted only a year, from October 1909 until Tolstoy's death in November 1910, but led Gandhi to give the name Tolstoy Colony to his second <a href="/wiki/Ashram" title="Ashram">ashram</a> in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> Both men also believed in the merits of <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarianism</a>, the subject of several of Tolstoy's essays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tolstoy also became a major supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a> movement. He was impressed by the pacifist beliefs of the <a href="/wiki/Doukhobors" title="Doukhobors">Doukhobors</a> and brought their persecution to the attention of the international community, after they burned their weapons in peaceful protest in 1895. He aided the Doukhobors to migrate to Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> He also provided inspiration to the <a href="/wiki/Mennonites" title="Mennonites">Mennonites</a>, another religious group with anti-government and anti-war sentiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> In 1904, Tolstoy condemned the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> and wrote to the Japanese Buddhist priest <a href="/wiki/Soyen_Shaku" title="Soyen Shaku">Soyen Shaku</a> in a failed attempt to make a joint pacifist statement.
</p><p>Towards the end of his life, Tolstoy become occupied with the economic theory and social philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Georgism" title="Georgism">Georgism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> He incorporated it approvingly into works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Resurrection_(Tolstoy_novel)" title="Resurrection (Tolstoy novel)">Resurrection</a></i> (1899), the book that was a major cause for his excommunication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> He spoke with great admiration of <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>, stating once that "People do not argue with the teaching of George; they simply do not know it. And it is impossible to do otherwise with his teaching, for he who becomes acquainted with it cannot but agree."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> He also wrote a preface to George's journal <i><a href="/wiki/Social_Problems" title="Social Problems">Social Problems</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> Tolstoy and George both rejected private property in land (the most important source of income for Russian aristocracy that Tolstoy heavily criticized). They also rejected a centrally planned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">socialist</a> economy. Because Georgism requires an administration to collect <a href="/wiki/Economic_rent" title="Economic rent">land rent</a> and spend it on infrastructure, some assume that this embrace moved Tolstoy away from his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a> views. However, anarchist versions of Georgism have been proposed since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> Tolstoy's 1899 novel <i>Resurrection</i> explores his thoughts on Georgism and hints that Tolstoy had such a view. It suggests small communities with local governance to manage the collective land rents for common goods, while still heavily criticising <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">state</a> institutions such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justice_system" title="Justice system">justice system</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4273" data-file-width="7596" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg/220px-Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg/330px-Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg/440px-Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tula_YasnayaPolyana_asv2019-09_img12_LNTolstoy_grave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tolstoy's grave with flowers at <a href="/wiki/Yasnaya_Polyana" title="Yasnaya Polyana">Yasnaya Polyana</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tolstoy died on 20 November, 1910 at the age of 82. Just before his death, his health was a concern of his family, who cared for him daily. In his last days, he spoke and wrote about dying. Renouncing his aristocratic lifestyle, he left home one winter night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> His secretive departure was an apparent attempt to escape from his wife's tirades. She spoke out against many of his teachings, and in recent years had grown envious of his attention to <a href="/wiki/Tolstoyan_movement" title="Tolstoyan movement">Tolstoyan</a> "disciples".
</p><p>Tolstoy died of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Lev_Tolstoy_(rural_locality)" title="Lev Tolstoy (rural locality)">Astapovo</a> railway station, after a day's train journey south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> The station master took Tolstoy to his apartment, and his personal doctors arrived and gave him injections of <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camphor" title="Camphor">camphor</a>.
</p><p>The police tried to limit access to his funeral procession, but thousands of peasants lined the streets. Still, some were heard to say that, other than knowing that "some nobleman had died", they knew little else about Tolstoy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to some sources, Tolstoy spent the last hours of his life preaching love, non-violence, and <a href="/wiki/Georgism" title="Georgism">Georgism</a> to fellow passengers on the train.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image"
