href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BC%27%D1%8F%D1%82%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA_.%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%81%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BC%D1%83_%D0%9B.%D0%9C,_%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%96.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Tolstoy in <a href="/wiki/Mariupol" title="Mariupol">Mariupol</a>, Ukraine, 2011</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg/190px-Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg/285px-Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg/380px-Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Munomento_Le%C3%B3n_Tolstoi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Tolstoy in <a href="/wiki/Montevideo" title="Montevideo">Montevideo</a>, Uruguay</div></div></div>
<p>Although Leo Tolstoy was regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Christian_anarchism" title="Christian anarchism">Christian anarchist</a> and not a socialist, his ideas and works still influenced socialist thinkers throughout history. He held an unromantic view of governments as being essentially violent forces held together by intimidation from state authority, corruption on behalf of officials, and the indoctrination of people from a young age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> In regard to his view of economics, he advocated for a return to <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">[89]</a></sup> In his view, a simplified economy would afford a lesser need for the exchange of goods, and as such, factories and cities – the centers of industry – would become obsolete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1944, literary historian and Soviet medievalist Nikolai Gudzii wrote a biography of Tolstoy that spanned 80 pages. It was designed to show readers that Tolstoy would have revised his pacifistic and anti-patriotic sentiments if he were alive amid World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> At around the same time, literary scholar and historian <a href="/wiki/Boris_Eikhenbaum" title="Boris Eikhenbaum">Boris Eikhenbaum</a> – in a stark contrast from his earlier works on Tolstoy – portrayed the Russian novelist as someone whose ideas aligned with those of early utopian socialists such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Owen" title="Robert Owen">Robert Owen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Saint-Simon" title="Henri de Saint-Simon">Henri Saint-Simon</a>. Eikenbaum suggested that these influences can be seen in Tolstoy's emphases on individual happiness and peasant welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-93">[91]</a></sup> The discrepancies in Eikenbaum's portrayals of Tolstoy can be attributed to the political pressure in Soviet Russia at the time: public officials pressured literary scholars to conform with party doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Soviet_Russia">In Soviet Russia</span></h3>
<p>From Tolstoy's writings the <a href="/wiki/Tolstoyan_movement" title="Tolstoyan movement">Tolstoyan movement</a> was birthed, and its members used his works to promote non-violence, anti-urbanism and opposition to the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> While Tolstoy himself never associated with the movement, as he was opposed to joining any organization or group, he named his thirteenth daughter <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Tolstaya" title="Alexandra Tolstaya">Alexandra (Sasha) L'vovna Tolstaya</a> the heir to his works with the intention that she would publish them for the Russian people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Tolstoy designated <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Chertkov" title="Vladimir Chertkov">Vladimir Chertkov</a> – who kept many of Tolstoy's manuscripts – as the editor of his works. Originally Tolstoy wanted to make the Russian people the heirs to his writings, but Russian law at the time decreed that property could only be inherited by one individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a> in 1917, writings that were formerly censored could now be published, since the all literary works were nationalized in November 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> Alexandra worked during these years to publish sets of Tolstoy's works: from 1917 to 1919, she worked with Zadruga Publishing House to publish thirteen booklets on Tolstoy's writings, which had previously been censored under Russia's imperial rule. However, publishing a complete collection of Tolstoy's works proved to be more difficult. In December 1918, the Commissariat of Education granted Chertkov a 10 million rouble subsidy to publish a complete edition of his works, but it never materialized due to government control of publication rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> Cooperatives were additionally made illegal in Russia in 1921, creating another obstacle for Alexandra and Chertkov.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-5"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1920s, Tolstoy's estate, <a href="/wiki/Yasnaya_Polyana" title="Yasnaya Polyana">Yasnaya Polyana</a>, was sanctioned by the Soviet regime to exist as a commune for Tolstoyans. The regime permitted this Christian-oriented community because they felt as if religious sects like the Tolstoyans were models for the Russian peasantry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-6"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> The Soviet government owned the estate, which was deemed a memorial for the late Russian writer, but Alexandra had jurisdiction over the education offered at Yasnaya Polyana. Unlike most Soviet schools, the schooling at Yasnaya Polyana did not offer militaristic training and did spread atheistic propaganda. Over time, though, local communists – as opposed to the state government, which financially supported the institution – often denounced the estate and called for frequent inspections. After 1928, a change in cultural policy in the Soviet regime led to a takeover of local institutions, including Tolstoy's estate. When Alexandra stepped down from her role as head of Yasnaya Poliana in 1929, the Commissariat of Education and Health took control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-7"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1925, the Soviet government created its first Jubilee Committee to celebrate the centennial of Tolstoy's birth, which originally consisted of 13 members but grew to 38 members after a second committee formed in 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-8"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> Alexandra was not content with the funds provided by the government, and met with Stalin in June 1928. During the meeting, Stalin said the government could not provide the one million roubles requested by the committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-9"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> However, an agreement was reached with the State Publishing House in April 1928 for the publishing of a 92-volume collection of Tolstoy's works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-10"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> During the Jubilee Celebration, <a href="/wiki/Anatoly_Lunacharsky" title="Anatoly Lunacharsky">Anatoly Luncharsky</a> – the head of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Commissariat_for_Education" title="People's Commissariat for Education">People's Commissariat for Education</a> – gave a speech in which he refuted reports that claimed the Soviet government was hostile towards Tolstoy and his legacy. Instead of focusing on the aspects of Tolstoy's works that pitted him against the Soviet regime, he instead focused on the unifying aspects, such as Tolstoy's love for equality and labor as well as his disdain for the state and private property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-11"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[92]</a></sup> More than 400 million copies of Tolstoy's works have been printed in the Soviet Union, making him the best-selling author in Soviet Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTIMES1987_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTIMES1987-95">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> wrote several essays about Tolstoy, suggesting that a contradiction exists within his critique of Russian society. According to Lenin, Tolstoy – who adored the peasantry and voiced their discontent with imperial Russian society – may have been revolutionary in his critiques, but his political consciousness was not fully developed for a revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-96">[94]</a></sup> Lenin uses this line of thinking to suggest that the <a href="/wiki/1905_Russian_Revolution" title="1905 Russian Revolution">1905 Russian Revolution</a>, which he called a "peasant bourgeois revolution," failed because of its backwardness: the revolutionaries wanted to dismantle the existing medieval forms of oppression and replace them with an old and patriarchal village-commune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-96">[94]</a></sup> Tolstoy's concept of non-resistance to evil additionally hindered the 1905 revolution's success, Lenin thought, because the movement was not militant and had thus allowed the autocracy to crush them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-96">[94]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Stalin concludes in his writings that despite the many contradictions in Tolstoy's critiques, his hatred for feudalism and capitalism mark the prelude to proletarian socialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-96">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, Tolstoy's philosophy of non-resistance to evil made an impact on <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi's</a> political thinking. Gandhi was deeply moved by Tolstoy's concept of truth, which, in his view, constitutes any doctrine that reduces suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[95]</a></sup> For both Gandhi and Tolstoy, truth is God, and since God is universal love, truth must therefore also be universal love. The Gujarati word for Gandhi's non-violent movement is "satyagraha," derived from the word "sadagraha" – the "sat" portion translating to "truth," and the "agraha" translating to "firmness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[95]</a></sup> Gandhi's conception of satyagraha was birthed from Tolstoy's understanding of Christianity, rather than from Hindu tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_films">In films</span></h3>
<p>A 2009 film about Tolstoy's final year, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Station" title="The Last Station">The Last Station</a></i>, based on the 1990 novel by <a href="/wiki/Jay_Parini" title="Jay Parini">Jay Parini</a>, was made by director <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Hoffman_(American_director)" title="Michael Hoffman (American director)">Michael Hoffman</a> with <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Plummer" title="Christopher Plummer">Christopher Plummer</a> as Tolstoy and <a href="/wiki/Helen_Mirren" title="Helen Mirren">Helen Mirren</a> as Sofya Tolstoya. Both performers were nominated for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Oscars</a> for their roles. There have been other films about the writer, including <i><a href="/wiki/Departure_of_a_Grand_Old_Man" title="Departure of a Grand Old Man">Departure of a Grand Old Man</a></i>, made in 1912 just two years after his death, <i>How Fine, How Fresh the Roses Were</i> (1913), and <i><a href="/wiki/Lev_Tolstoy_(film)" title="Lev Tolstoy (film)">Lev Tolstoy</a></i>, directed by and starring <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Gerasimov_(film_director)" title="Sergei Gerasimov (film director)">Sergei Gerasimov</a> in 1984.
</p><p>There is also a famous <a href="/wiki/Lost_film" title="Lost film">lost film</a> of Tolstoy made a decade before he died. In 1901, the American travel lecturer <a href="/wiki/Burton_Holmes" title="Burton Holmes">Burton Holmes</a> visited Yasnaya Polyana with <a href="/wiki/Albert_J._Beveridge" title="Albert J. Beveridge">Albert J. Beveridge</a>, the U.S. senator and historian. As the three men conversed, Holmes filmed Tolstoy with his 60-mm movie camera. Afterwards, Beveridge's advisers succeeded in having the film destroyed, fearing that the meeting with the Russian author might hurt Beveridge's chances of running for the U.S. presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
